Roborally a vadnyugaton. Ez a legegyszerűbb kifejezés, ami illik a játékra. Van egy kezdő paklija minden rosszfiúnak és hat tölténye, amivel egy vonatot próbálnak kirabolni. A játékosszámtól függően van adott számú kocsi, egy mozdony és egy seriff a vonaton. Magában a kocsikban és azok tetején lehet garázdálkodni. Célunk a legtöbb értéket összeszedni. Ebben a játékban nem pakliépítés, hanem paklirombolás folyik, ugyanis jobb lapokat nem kapunk, csak nem használható lövéseket az ellenfeleinktől, amik nehezítik a jó lapok használatát játék közben. Ha kézitusára kerül sor, akkor még egy értékünk is elveszik. Gyönyörű kivitel, emiatt nagyon jól beleélheti magát a játékos a vadnyugati hangulatba. RATING: 5.5
1 play (Origins 2015)

Our explainer said there were 2 ways to play the order cards: all in one stack, or each player in their own area (like RoboRally). We played the latter (none of us wanted to add a memory element to the game) but several people later told me that the game is MUCH better with the former -- more chaos and "fun". 

Our game, playing the cards RoboRally style, was just OK. We still had some chaos thanks to the face up/down card plays, but we were each mostly able to do what we wanted. For me, that's good: I don't like chaos just for the sake of chaos. But the game just wasn't all that much fun! Playing cards to one stack would simply add a memory element to the game, which I think would just be annoying. I would much rather play RoboRally, with players planning simultaneously under time pressure and then seeing what happens.

Nice-looking components (although the train cars don't exactly lock together well and are easy to displace) but not much game here. I guess I'd try it again with the cards-in-one-stack rule, but I won't go looking.

[b]--- Rules Summary ---[/b]
The "board" is actually a 3D train with several cars, each with one space inside and another on top (on the roof). Players each play a different cowboy/girl bandit, with a unique power. Place your meeple in the train, along with the sheriff and a mix of random gem/money sacks worth $250-500 each. Each (of 4) round, players draw a random hand of 6 from their deck of programming cards. 

In turn order, place an order face up or down (depends on the setup card drawn for this round, modified by any character-specific powers) into a stack -- four or five times depending on the round card. After all orders are placed, they are resolved in the order played. Orders include things like: moving forward within the train or going up/down to/from roof, punching (someone in the same car as you), shooting (into next car, or anywhere on roof), pick up loot, move the sheriff, etc. If you end up in the same car as the sheriff, he shoots you. Whenever you're hit, you add a damage card to your deck and drop one of your loot tokens.

After four rounds, the player with the highest total value loot wins! 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a programmed movement game where each player plays a bandit on a train trying to get the most loot while shooting and punching their opponents to prevent them from grabbing loot and avoiding the sheriff.

I still haven't really landed on my opinion of this game yet.  At face value I really wouldn't normally like this game.  It is pure uncontrollable chaos a lot of times and while there were a couple moments where I was able to properly plan my moves to grab a high value piece of loot there were also rounds where I shot empty air and flailed my arms wildly at nothing.  There is certainly a big toy factor with the 3D train but that's not something I really care a lot about.  I like it better for that than if it was a 2D board but I would never hold onto a game just because of that.

I do think there are some neat things here like the ability to occasionally play cards hidden due to tunnels but for the most part I felt like I had no control and was just watching the game unfold.  But the game that did unfold was pretty fun.  Much in the same way I can get enjoyment out of a random unpredicted turn in RoboRally I can enjoy this game when my best laid plans end with me flailing my arms wildly in an empty train car.  The theme is pretty prevalent and it doesn't overstay its welcome, playing really quickly and accessible enough to teach anyone inside 10 minutes.

There are even some serious balance issues, I think the power for Belle to not be the target if there is another legal target is pretty much the best one of the lot.  Especially with larger player counts where I think this game is most fun even if that increases the chaos factor but I think that's how this game has to be played.  A filler where you can turn your brain off mostly and have fun with it.  Although that novelty may wear off before long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Programmed actions a la Roborally but moving meeples around a fantastical cardboard train. Players take turns playing cards, some face up and some face down, showing actions like "move to/from the roof of the train", "shoot", "punch someone", "move along the train" or "move the marshal". If another player's action causes them to be somewhere else -- for example, a punch will cause them to reel back into an adjacent carriage -- then they might find themselves shooting the wrong person.

For all that much of the fun is in seeing people's plans ruined, it's just as satisfying to correctly read opponents such that a plan does come off. The combination is delightful. It is at times vexing that taking actions requires drawing the right card, particularly after you have been shot a few times (bullets clog up your hand). That sort of denial isn't nearly as fun.

Update: I am increasingly unhappy with the bullet mechanism; it's entertaining to have plans ruined by an unexpected marshal (or similar), not so much to require turns wasted drawing cards in order to achieve [i]anything[/i]. Perhaps a variant where they just drop loot would work, as when punched.

This smacks of "we need an add-on mechanic to get people to be more aggressive."  As a funny, fairly quick "let's see what happens" game, it's okay, but there are still plenty of games in that genre with much better gameplay.

Tom compared this unfavourably to River Dragons and I think he's absolutely right; River Dragons is prettier, much more fun and allows for much more entertaining dicking over of other players.

I suspect that the 3D train will keep this popular and it'd make a decent gateway game, but there's little to keep me coming back to it.

RATING: 4
In a word, lackluster. It's cute, but feels more like a toy than a game. Yes, the components are 3D, and yes it comes with terrain elements that have no effect other than ambiance, but what I actually mean is that the game rules seem like an excuse to play with all those beautiful bits.

It's not that he game isn't fun, but rather that it's very light and chaotic. At no point did I feel much engagement. Programmed movement done right can be a very engaging and rewarding mechanism, but here it felt as though the designer wasn't taking full advantage of its potential. Rather than struggling to program a series of meaningful actions, I felt as though I was merely moving things around and occasionally gathering up some victory points. Decision points felt sort of empty, as though it was the theme that was supposed to carry the weight of enjoyment.

I was happy when the game was over, and I don't plan to play ever again. Even as a family game, I wonder if the novelty would quickly wear off. Oh, and the 3D elements actually became a nuisance as I struggled to pick up and move small playing pieces to the correct location on the train.

RATING: 8.7
I'm not a massive advocate for theme, but this game oozes theme. Not only are the 3D trains fun, but the programming actually gives me the feeling of a chaotic brawl, as the characters frantically struggle to steal the loot while fighting off one another on a moving train. I'm not sure I've felt as engaged with a game's theme via it's mechanics as I have this one, and when you combine that with the insanely good production, this is why I rate it so high. It's a joy to play, and makes me want to try more programming games, as it could be a mechanic I find to be very immersive.

RATING: 7.5

This and the fact that the game plays well up to 6 makes this one a winner for me. The western theme is icing on the cake.

Colt Express also finishes in under an hour which is a great timeframe for this type of game.

RoboRally is still the greatest though.  : )




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 2
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 5

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A light game that uses stacked actions in a very fun way.

The production values are very high in this one, and the whole train robbery theme carries on very well. The main action stacking mechanism creates player chaos instead of luck, but to a manageable level which makes it fun. The extra player abilities are also a nice touch, and there is enough agony and hidden cash to keep all the players involved to the end.

There is little to whine about this game, other than it tries to pass itself for more that what it really is. For a light fun game it takes too long to setup, and I can see it after a few games when the novelty wears out to slightly outstay its welcome. The theme is also not my cup of tea, as it is both too narrow (just a train robbery) and too distant (western but without the "settling" feeling)

RATING: 7
Someone described this game as "RoboRally meets Bang" and while there's some truth to that, I think this game is vastly better than either of those games.

The game is about a train robbery. All the players are bandits stealing the loot from a train... but we're not particularly on the same side. There's a very cute train assembly that allows players to put their meeple in or on top of the train. There's random look tokens scattered about the train, as well as one marshall.

You have a deck of cards, of which you'll draw six for each round. On a round, you first reveal the play sequence card which shows how many cards you get to play in the round and whether they'll be played publicly or secretly. So the sequence might say that everyone plays a card, then everyone plays two cards in rapid succession, then everyone plays a card secretly and finally everyone plays a card, but in reverse turn order. After all the cards are played the deck is flipped over and all the actions are carried out in deck order. So there's a "programming" aspect where you are trying to plan out what's going to happen based on what you see other people are doing.

So what can you do? First, you can move. There are two types of move cards, vertical movement or horizontal. If you're inside the train, you can move to the next train (horizontal) or up to the roof (vertical). If you're on the roof you can move up to 3 train cars (horizontal) or down to the train car (vertical). One thing that keeps the game from being really random is that you don't have to committ whether you move towards the front or back of the train until the card is revealed in the execute phase of the turn. So if things didn't go how you thought, you can change the direction you thought you wanted to go.

You can also pick up loot. There's three types of loot... briefcases ($1000), gems ($500) and bags of loot ($250-$500). Or you can punch someone causing them to drop a piece of loot, you choose which one, but you can't peek to see how much the bags of loot are.

Or you can shoot someone. If you're in the train, you can only shoot someone in the next car over. If you're on top of the train you can shoot someone who is also on top of the train, but as far away as you like, provided that there's no one in between the two of you. Shooting someone means you give them a bullet card which just clogs their deck.

You can also move the sheriff one car over (he never goes on the roof). If you're ever in the same car as the sheriff, you get shot (taking a bullet from the sheriff) and flee to the roof.

Then you reset for the next round, shuffle your deck and start over, with your meeples wherever you left them and with whatever loot hasn't been grabbed as yet.

Every player has a special power, which makes the game a little more interesting. Some powers, like not being able to be targeted for shots and punches if you're standing next to someone else, or automatically grabbing the loot when you punch someone else, seem to be nicer than some of the other powers, like drawing seven cards instead of six.

The game is a little crazy, but not nearly as much so as most of the other "program your move ahead of time" games. And it's quick and fairly fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[thing=158899][/thing] is a simple game that involves action programming: one card is revealed, at the start of every round (the play lasts for 5 rounds) and it tells the players the amount of cards that will be played in the round, how the Marshal will act, and a few quirks - like, after 1 turn of each player, the playing order reverses, or there will be a turn when everyone will play the card hidden.

Each players has 10 action cards and drawrs, at the start of each round, 6 of them. There aren't 10 different actions, as 4 of them have two cards and the Punch and the Marshal are unique. The actions are:

- Move (x2): to 1 adjacent cart if inside the train, or up to three carts if outside the train;
- Fire (x2): hits one player one cart away (inside the train) or whatever distance if in the ceiling of the train;
- Robbery (x2): takes one of the items in the cart;
- Floor Change (x2): allows to move to the roof or get down from it, back inside the train;
- Punch (x1): hits someone in the same cart - the other character drops one item in the cart and is moved to an adjacent cart;
- Marshal (x1): moves the Marshal to an adjacent cart.

Players will keep choosing actions, trying to compensate for the actions chosen by the others, vying for the best opportunities. For instance, lets say your robber is in the roof of the train, with the Marshal in the cart bellow. Your idea could be to use your first the Marshal, to move him away, then the second a Down card, as the third will be a Robbery card. But after you put your second card (Down) someone, after you, put a Marshal. Now he can come back and destroy your stealing bonanza. Will you risk now the Robbery card (as the Marshal could be used to move further away), or is better to just put a Fire card, to at least have some proper retribution (while also trying to take the Braggart bonus at the end of the game)?

In the same vein, a player could, as her first card, put a Robbery. However, someone else, with his character in the same cart, puts a Punch. It was a risky decision, but with only one Punch, and drawing 6 out of 10 cards, it wal possible that the player wouldn't have a Punch in hand. Now, if she has the second Robbery card in hand, she can try again, but she can't know to which cart she will be moved to!

This is the game dynamics: action cards are added to a deck, in play order, until everyone had the amount of turns showing in the round card. Then, in order, from the first card played to the last one, actions will be activated. Yes, it is somewhat easy to plan when you can see everyone else's cards. But one character, the Ghost, plays his first card face down. And the round cards also insert turns in which everyone will put their cards face down. Then madness can issue! In your play, for instance, one player used two Robbery cards in the roof of the train, gaining nothing, after being sent there by a movement of the Marshal. Definitely a fun sight to behold!

It is worth mentioning that the players assume the role of characters, each with an unique ability. The Ghost, as I said, play his first card face down. The Doc draws 7 cards instead of 6. Belle, if there is another legal target, can't be hit by a Punch or a Fire. Django, when he Fires, he moves the target 1 cart away. Tuco can Fire someone in the same cart or in the roof. This surely adds to the replay value of the game.

Each time someone gets shot, the person adds a Fire card to her deck. So, at first, everyone has 10 action cards in the deck, but as the play goes by, after some shootouts, some decks are polluted with Fire cards and the 6 card draw at the start of the round may leave someone with less than 6 actions (we had turns were someone had only 3, the other 3 beign Fire cards). To help get somewhat around this, the player can use one action to draw 3 cards instead of adding a card to the planning deck, so that, in her next turn, the person will have more options available.

The goal of the game is to have the highest amount of money at the end of 5 rounds of play - usually the best way to do this is by stealing. There are three main items to steal in the train: briefcase (worth $1000), diamonds (worth $500) and money bags (worth from $250 to $500, but this information is hidden until the player takes the bag). Also the player that shot more gains the Braggart bonus, which is worth $1000. Finally, there are some other ways to gain money, due to requirements of the round (event) cards - for instance, all the characters that are in the Locomotive by the end of the round gains $250.

So [thing=158899][/thing] is a fun and light game, easy to teach and to play. The level of the production is really high, with good art and a 3D train - the game even comes with some totally unecessary and cool extras, only there to help set the mood of the theme, with cactus, thornbush and rock mounts. The meeples are also thematic, with large brim hats and pistols. 

There are some degree of bastardness in the game, with the Punches, shots and movements of the Marshal, but, overall, the play happens in a pretty light spirit, all beign mainly for fun, not to be taken too seriously. There is also a high level of luck present in the mechanics, and you have to be aware of this to have a good relation with [thing=158899][/thing]. I can imagine that some player might "freeze" when trying to consider the effect of what she will play together with everything that have already beign played (and could have been), leading to some AP or basically chaotic play with no thought whatsoever. It is a risk, though not too present nor really dangerous.

Overall, [thing=158899][/thing] pleased me, showing itself as a good option to accommodate 4 to 6 players (the game accepts less - 2 and 3 - but it seemed to me that a good deal of the fun would be lost when playing with lower counts). A play will last around 30 to 40 minutes. Final words: I recommended it!

RATING: 9
This game is a pure fun :).

Yes - it is chaotic, yes - it is unbalanced, yes - it is mostly a game of chance, yes - some of player's decisions are a bit negligible. As a serious game it has a lot of problems... but wait, it's not a serious game :P.

I played it several times, didn't win a single game and still love it. I love the Old West theme, the artwork, awesome 3D train and overall quality of components (cactus decorations!).

The game mechanics gives you an exact feeling of a train robbery kerfuffle that you may have seen in all this Spaghetti Western movies. There is no time for strict planning, you have to act and be ready for sudden and unexpected consequences.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I liked the theme of this game when I saw it being played at my local board games club a while back. The train robbery is a classic western film material and each player portrays a 'typical' character from the era. I think at least one of the characters - the showgirl (?) does not carry a gun and this might frustrate some people who are expecting a deluge of gunfire! The mechanics are straightforward although I did expect to have to move outside of a railcar before going up onto the roof which confused me initially. The ten cards each character has form a draw deck, and from memory, you draw six and play these in a sequence in accordance with a template deck at the side of the cardboard train. You can move, climb, punch, shoot or move the sheriff. There were a few interesting 'double backs' when moving this character away from the strongroom. The starting positions of the characters are set, if I understand correctly, so being in a rail car with no other starting robbers is an advantage. That said, as I had the 'Ghost' character, he has the ability of not needing to declare his first card face up. Those with a good memory will be better able to recall the sequence of what other players are doing. Punching successfully means the character punched drops whatever they are carrying allowing the 'old switcheroo' to be done and a quick escape made. I don't like the idea the restrictions on shooting railcar to railcar but accept the game has been simplified to make it easier and quicker to play. I think that an advanced version of the game would suit me better with a few more options once the basics are acquired. It may be that this has been addressed in the expansion sets. I made a few wrong choices but felt this game was good nonetheless and those I played with entered into the 'cinematic' experience in the right way. A good game for me!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I’ve enjoyed RoboRally in the past, but had soured on it because of it’s length. I didn’t mind the frustration of the game because it was so silly, but taking 2+ hours to play a game of this weight was far too long. Colt Express came along promising a game with similar game play, but a defined game length and fewer options to get tangled in.

In Colt Express you’re a train robber moving from car to car (these are 3D train cars) and stealing purses, jewels, and the strong box in order to have the most money at the end of five rounds. You start each round with a hand of 6 cards that let you move between train cards, move into or on top of a train car, move the marshall, pick up treasure, punch another player (making them drop a purse), or shoot another player (putting a dead card into their deck). You’ll put anywhere from 4-6 cards, typically face up, into a pile one at a time and then that pile is turned over and each card is played one at a time. Sometimes you’ll play the cards face down, sometimes you’ll play two cards at once, and sometimes you’ll play in reverse player order. At the end of your five rounds, whoever has the most money wins.

I think this largely replaces RoboRally for me, and that’s a tough realization. I really like what RoboRally does and how funny it is, but it’s just too long for what it is and ends up becoming frustrating as a result. Colt Express on the other hand, lives up to it’s name, playing in about 45 minutes to an hour while giving roughly the same feeling.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is fantastic!  Great player interaction and a lot of fun trying to work your own strategy while avoiding attacks from other players.  You're all simultaneously trying to rob the amazing 3D train, but each player wants to be the richest bandit so friendly fire may cause you to drop some loot.

The game occurs in 5 rounds of 2 phases each.  The first phase has players planning their actions for the round in turn order.  The actions then occur in the second phase.  Each round consists of 4 or 5 turns with various twists to how cards are played, and every round ends with a random event.  Some rounds let you play a card face down, others let you play 2 cards in a row.  The action planning mechanic allows you to get an idea of what is going to happen, but the action resolution never goes exactly as you'd expect.  The richest bandit at the end of the game wins!

Some of the variable player powers are incredibly unbalanced, so we tend to not play with them.

I like pre-programming games, and this was fun and different... and so very over produced! :laugh:  I thought being able to see parts of people's plans as they laid our their cards was a pretty neat twist so you could react to what they were doing... and it made being able to correctly deduce their hidden cards that much more rewarding.  Of course there are lots of funny (and I suppose for some people frustrating) moments when you are punching at air after somebody moved or a whole turn is out the window because somebody does something surprising with the Sherrif, but if you take it lightly and have fun with it Colt Express can be a good time.

RATING: 4
The train is awesome, if, in practice, a bit small for actually getting bits into and out of, if you are not a small child. The thematic aspects of the game work pretty nicely; however, the lack of control is not good. The card use and programming takes cues from both [thing=18]RoboRally[/thing] and [thing=49]Mamma Mia![/thing] / [thing=13530]Sole Mio![/thing], with a dash of deck-[-]building[/-]wrecking, yet ends up having the control in Mamma Mia! / Sole Mio!. It. Is. Not. Fun. To program your moves when it is literally impossible — even without opponents messing things up — to get anything or shoot anyone turn after turn. Give the players their whole deck, and force them to choose what's going into the discard, and this game would be at least a [b][bgcolor=#9999ff] 6 [/bgcolor][/b]; as it is, I did not have fun, in spite of good company and cool bits.

I did not have high expectations for this game, because it had such an awesome (and gimmicky) look; however, it did not live up to even those fairly neutral expectations, for me. May you enjoy it anyway!
